  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_c7321999-12b2-4dd7-b8c3-f42abc0966ad'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer976966439429777828">The first book in Louise Erdrich's highly acclaimed "Native American" trilogy that includes "The Beet Queen," "Tracks," and "The Bingo Palace," re-sequenced and expanded to include never-before-published chapters.<br/><br/>Set on and around a North Dakota Ojibwe reservation, <i>Love Medicine</i> is the epic story about the intertwined fates of two families: the Kashpaws and the Lamartines.</span>
  <span id="freeText976966439429777828" style="display:none">The first book in Louise Erdrich's highly acclaimed "Native American" trilogy that includes "The Beet Queen," "Tracks," and "The Bingo Palace," re-sequenced and expanded to include never-before-published chapters.<br /><br />Set on and around a North Dakota Ojibwe reservation, <i>Love Medicine</i> is the epic story about the intertwined fates of two families: the Kashpaws and the Lamartines.<br /><br />With astonishing virtuosity, each chapter draws on a range of voices to limn its tales. Black humor mingles with magic, injustice bleeds into betrayal, and through it all, bonds of love and family marry the elements into a tightly woven whole that pulses with the drama of life.<br /><br />Filled with humor, magic, injustice and betrayal, Erdrich blends family love and loyalty in a stunning work of dramatic fiction.</span>
  <a data-text-id="976966439429777828" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1813541563" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1813541563" />
    <a title="Brina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2933855-brina"><img alt="Brina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601675085p2/2933855.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1813541563?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brina" href="/user/show/2933855-brina">Brina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=national-book-award">national-book-award</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=native-american">native-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=rereads">rereads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=500-great-books-women">500-great-books-women</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1813541563" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2900857860532442810">A member of the Chippewa and Obijwe tribes, Louise Erdrich has been a leading voice in Native American literature for over thirty years. Determined to publish her first book before she turned thirty, Erdrich wrote Love Medicine at the age of twenty nine, and this debut novel won the National Critics Circle Book Award. Following the intricate web woven by the Kapshaw and Nanapush families over the course of fifty years, Erdrich creates real characters that tug on the heartstrings of human emotion</span>
  <span id="freeText2900857860532442810" style="display:none">A member of the Chippewa and Obijwe tribes, Louise Erdrich has been a leading voice in Native American literature for over thirty years. Determined to publish her first book before she turned thirty, Erdrich wrote Love Medicine at the age of twenty nine, and this debut novel won the National Critics Circle Book Award. Following the intricate web woven by the Kapshaw and Nanapush families over the course of fifty years, Erdrich creates real characters that tug on the heartstrings of human emotions. <br /><br />Albertine Johnson has returned to the reservation for the funeral of her Aunt June Morrissey. In this opening chapter we meet her grandparents Nector and Marie Kapshaw and their descendants. Alcoholism is a persistent problem amongst the men, and there is little that the women of the tribe can do to prevent it. We see this especially from Albertine's cousin King Kapshaw who drinks rampantly because he feels that because he married an outsider, that he can get away with drinking. Because of the drinking issues, there have been illicit extra marital affairs, creating a reservation where most people are loosely related to one another. <br /><br />This web of relationships can be traced to Nector Kapshaw who loved two women over his entire life: his wife Marie Kapshaw nee Lazarre and vixen Lulu Lamartine. Between the two women he fathered eight children, six surviving. The Kapshaw and Lamartine blood continued to intermix, creating an environment where strong Indian blood flowed through all the characters veins. Even though drinking and affairs are prevalent on Indian reservations, the women do not condone the behavior. Marie and Lulu remained rivals for Nestor's behavior for his entire life and there was little love for each other even though they moved in the same circles. <br /><br />Erdrich does a masterful job of painting a picture of Native American traditions and beliefs. She briefly touches on the northern lights celebration, her people's lack of trust of white Americans and their forming a tribal council to voice their concern for their rights, and role alcoholism plays amongst natives on reservations who have little else to do with their time. Written in vignette form, Erdrich gives a voice to many characters in three generations of Nanapush/Lamartine and Kapshaw members of the Chippewa tribe. Each character is unique even though they share many of the same genetics and environment. As a result many issues repeat themselves over the course of the fifty years related in the novel. <br /><br />Since the first publication of Love Medicine, Louise Erdrich has gone to write many novels, all focusing on Native American issues. The characters in this first novel can be found in four other of her novels, as Erdrich paints a more vivid picture of their backstories. As she became more successful, Erdrich founded Birchbark Books, with all of the proceeds returning to native Americans. Love Medicine started her career and I enjoyed the imagery and powerful characterizations in this debut novel, which I rate 4.5 bright stars. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2900857860532442810" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1032477499" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1032477499" />
    <a title="Zanna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna"><img alt="Zanna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512813535p2/22591633.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1032477499?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zanna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zanna" href="/user/show/22591633-zanna">Zanna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/6484819-love-medicine">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=2015year-reading-women">2015year-reading-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=bechdel-pass">bechdel-pass</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22591633-zanna?shelf=native">native</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1032477499" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7282822583629981865">Sometimes the books I enjoy most are the ones I have the least to say about. And what can I add to Toni Morrison's comment that "the beauty of <i>Love Medicine</i> saves us from being completely devastated by its power"? Because reading this book is living, in sweetness and beauty and love, even when it tells terrible things.<br/><br/>It's life and there are as may ways of looking at it as there are minds to see, but in so far as these folks have been and still are fighting for survival, not just of the individu</span>
  <span id="freeText7282822583629981865" style="display:none">Sometimes the books I enjoy most are the ones I have the least to say about. And what can I add to Toni Morrison's comment that "the beauty of <i>Love Medicine</i> saves us from being completely devastated by its power"? Because reading this book is living, in sweetness and beauty and love, even when it tells terrible things.<br /><br />It's life and there are as may ways of looking at it as there are minds to see, but in so far as these folks have been and still are fighting for survival, not just of the individual bodies but ways of being alive together and the deathlessness of stories. It's a fight fought ducking and rolling and with tricks of all styles, with 'one paw tied behind my back'. Sometimes it's fought by going with the flow, by listening to the heart or the spirit or the craving of flesh, and seeking what's wanted. Sometimes it's fought in humility or by letting go, sometimes by audacity and pride in the face of censure. There are losses and grief, but the dead travel with the living. <br /><br />I know it's life because Erdrich's approach to character is to call people into being and tell their stories as they come to her. The structure here is not beginning middle end but stretched in directions of flow, wandering, straight, circuitous. Rise, fall, in, out, up, down, under and behind, around over and through, branchings and remeetings. It is a riversong, speaking all seasons and all weathers, telling melodies of snow and starlight, drought and storm. You can jump in anywhere anytime and feel the voices of Erdrich's people, feel their loves and ties.<br /><br />Erdrich and her characters deal with racism and colonisation with a wry attitude. I had to laugh as well as sigh at the 'The Plunge of the Brave', Nector Kashpaw's account of the modelling and acting opportunities offered to him that inscribe ever deeper the mythology of the vanishing native. Endless facsimilies of his image dying in regalia could be exhaled into the already poisoned cultural atmosphere.<br /><br />This text is allusive, rich in the symbolic, like the egg June accepts from a stranger in a bar (such openness! It speaks desperation but also the relentless will to survive and flourish somehow), or the huge baby, distilled from immense vivacity, weightless on the commercial scales. Its philosophical skein is stretched over a mystical Catholicism as well as Ojibwe culture and the hollowing horror of North American modernity in poverty. Each character-story has a place in the weave, and sees the lie of land and worldscape differently, and each has their own genius - for love, for getting money, for healing, for raising children:<blockquote>Lulu was bustling about the kitchen in a calm, automatic frenzy. She seemed to fill pots wth food by pointing at them and take things from the oven that she'd never put in. The table jumped to set itself. The pop foamed into glasses, and the milk sighed to the lip. The youngest boy, crushed in a high chair, watched eagerly while things placed themselves around him. Everyone sat down. The the boys began to stuff themselves with a savage and astonishing efficiency. Before Bev had cleaned his plate once, they'd had thirds, and by the time he looked up from dessert, they had melted through the walls. The youngest had levitated from his high chair and was sleeping out of sight. The room was empty except for Lulu and himself</blockquote>Even as I was reading I couldn't wait to read this again.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7282822583629981865" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_679313950" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/679313950" />
    <a title="Julie Christine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1213607-julie-christine"><img alt="Julie Christine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445900882p2/1213607.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/679313950?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie Christine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie Christine" href="/user/show/1213607-julie-christine">Julie Christine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060975814" href="/book/show/1087091.Love_Medicine">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=read-2013">read-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=usa-contemporary">usa-contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1213607-julie-christine?shelf=best-of-2013">best-of-2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer679313950" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9996084199156934035"><blockquote>Her clothes were filled with safety pins and hidden tears</blockquote><br/>Last week I sat on the steps of a downtown pier, stalled in the summer sun, reading my 1989 paperback edition of <i>Love Medicine</i>. With its Washington Husky-purple cover and title blaring in giant Brittanic Bold white font, the book must have appeared to the uninitiated like a pulp romance. Little did they know it was one of the most significant works of American fiction published in the 1980s, by an author who has become a national literary </span>
  <span id="freeText9996084199156934035" style="display:none"><blockquote>Her clothes were filled with safety pins and hidden tears</blockquote><br />Last week I sat on the steps of a downtown pier, stalled in the summer sun, reading my 1989 paperback edition of <i>Love Medicine</i>. With its Washington Husky-purple cover and title blaring in giant Brittanic Bold white font, the book must have appeared to the uninitiated like a pulp romance. Little did they know it was one of the most significant works of American fiction published in the 1980s, by an author who has become a national literary treasure. <br /><br />Louise Erdrich squeezes the back of our neck and pushes our resisting head to look directly into the lives of Native Americans on a reservation—a part of North American culture about which most of us know very little, segregated as reservations are by politics, geography, contempt, and pity. And the reader does more than observe—she sees, hears, thinks, feels, loves, and suffers as Erdrich’s characters do, through fifty years and the countless episodes of heartbreak, laughter, rage, and grace.<br /><br /><i>Love Medicine</i> opens in 1981 with the death of beautiful but broken June Kashpaw. June stumbles from a truck cab and runs from a stranger who calls her by another woman's name as he makes love to her. She sets out for her home on a North Dakota Chippewa reservation, following her instincts through a later winter storm. But her sharp survival skills, honed in a lifetime of living out-of-doors, cannot overpower the snowstorm or keep her warm in a pair of jeans and a thin jacket. <br /><br />June’s death propels the narrative down a path of memories connecting two Chippewa familes—the Kashpaws and the Lamartines. <i>Love Medicine</i> is the first in Erdrich’s symphony of novels featuring characters from the fictional town of Argus, North Dakota, set in and around the reservation. Although she dies in the story’s opening scene, June’s spirit holds the narrative together. The thread of her life is woven through each character’s story. <br /><br />The author uses a conversational first-person give the reader a sense of second skin with the characters. Mixed in are handful of third-person limited narratives that imbue the story with a lyrical, almost mythical tone. <br /><br />The writing is gorgeous. The characters are so vividly rendered, you feel them in your blood. <blockquote>She was a long-legged Chippewa woman, aged hard in every way except how she moved.</blockquote> AH! Could there be a more perfect sentence? <br /><blockquote>She was a natural blond with birdlike legs and, true, no chin, but great blue snapping eyes<br /><br />Gordie had dark, round, eager face, creased and puckered from being stitched up after an accident. His face was like something valuable that was broken and put carefully back together.</blockquote><br />Even as the characters speak directly to you, drawing you into their secret thoughts, shames and desires, Erdrich’s prose is like music, full of shifting tones and rhythms, crescendos and counterpoints. <br /><blockquote>Veils of love which was only hate petrified by longing--that was me.<br /><br />So many things in the world have happened before. But it's like they never did. Every new thing that happens to a person, it's a first... In that night I felt expansion, as if the world was branching out in shoots and growing faster than the eye could see. I felt smallness, how the earth divided into bits and kept dividing. I felt stars.<br /><br />There they were. And he was really loving her up good, boy, and she was going hell for leather. Sheets were flapping on the lines above and washcloths, pillowcases, shirts was also flying through the air, for they was trying to clear a place for themselves in a high-heaped but shallow laundry cart.</blockquote><br />There is evil and mystery, as Marie Lazarre escapes the horror of the convent on the hill in the 1930s; Sister Leopolda’s fingers like a “bundle of broom straws, her eye sockets two deep lashless hollows in a taut skull” will haunt your dreams. <br /><br />There are stories of betrayal: Nector Kashpaw turns away from his wife for the comfort of his first love, the easy, sensual Lulu Lamartine, mother of eight boys by eight fathers; June has an affair with tribal legend Gerry Nanapush, whose 6’3”, 250-pound frame cannot be contained by any prison, and leaves their son to be raised by the tribe as she had been. <br /><br />You will ache for Henry’s future, wasted in the jungles of Vietnam and pray that Albertine, the first of her family to attend university, doesn’t waste hers. There is deep despair, as Gordie, wretched with alcohol, hallucinates the deer he has hit is his dead wife, June. He bundles the deer into the back seat of his car and the scene which unfolds is sickening and desperately sad. <br /><br />And there is redemption and love, as tender and insightful Lipsha Morrisey, who isn’t aware until he is a grown man that June is his mother, finds a way to forgive and love the woman who cast him off; as Marie opens her home and heart to stray children; as two old women, enemies since childhood, come together in their final years. <br /><br />It is challenging to keep straight the shared bloodlines and histories. I believe later editions contain a family tree of sorts. But Erdrich explains these connected lives in a way that you realize they are like the root system of an aspen tree—one tree, standing alone, is really part of a vast forest:<br /><br /><blockquote>They moved in dance steps too intricate for the noninitiated eye to imitate or understand. Clearly they were of one soul. Handsome, rangy, wildly various, they were bound in total loyalty, not by oath, but by the simple, unquestioning belongingness of part of one organism.</blockquote><br />Whatever its flaws, and apparently Erdrich found enough to revise the book and publish new editions in recent years, <i>Love Medicine</i> is the reason we read: to be shaken to our core by characters we hate to leave behind as we turn the final pages. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9996084199156934035" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_18826827" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18826827" />
    <a title="Kirk" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/667059-kirk"><img alt="Kirk" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442453577p2/667059.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18826827?book_show_action=true">Mar 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kirk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kirk" href="/user/show/667059-kirk">Kirk</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/667059-kirk?shelf=currently-teaching">currently-teaching</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18826827" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5768525046246161903">Erdrich's first and still best-known work (because it's the one most often taught) has become something of a model for the contemporary short-story cycle, with interconnected stories devoted to a variety of interrelated characters spanning three (almost four) generations. The strength here is less in story (which centers on a love triangle and its effect on family ties) or character (vivid as they may be, they're still devoted women and unreliable men) than in style. I wouldn't call it lyrical b</span>
  <span id="freeText5768525046246161903" style="display:none">Erdrich's first and still best-known work (because it's the one most often taught) has become something of a model for the contemporary short-story cycle, with interconnected stories devoted to a variety of interrelated characters spanning three (almost four) generations. The strength here is less in story (which centers on a love triangle and its effect on family ties) or character (vivid as they may be, they're still devoted women and unreliable men) than in style. I wouldn't call it lyrical because Erdrich's prose isn't incantatory, but it is poetic, with densely woven metaphors and knockout word choices that are at once imagistic and yet hallucinatory. First-time readers will want to keep a genealogy handy because the names can get confusing (Gordie/Gerry, Lipsha/Lyman, etc). Perhaps the neatest thing about such a book is the way each subsequent story revises the preceding one through a contrasting point of view. My favorite moment is when Nector imagines his wife Marie as a fourteen year old girl beckoning him home while he stands outside his mistress Lulu's house in "The Plunge of the Brave." In the next story, "Flesh and Blood," we discover it's actually Nector and Marie's daughter, Zelda, who has gone to fetch her father before he abandons the family. I'm also partial to "Crossing the Water," the final selection, in which Lipsha meets his father, the legendary Native American trickster Gerry Nanapush. Their dialogue is awkward, surfacy, and yet somehow fulfilling and touching, providing just the right amount of resolution to a book whose energies are centrifugal instead of centripetal. The direction of those energies make it less satisfying if read as a novel; if read as a story cycle, however, it's an invigorating read that makes you want to write yourself. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5768525046246161903" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_224836687" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/224836687" />
    <a title="Jennifer (aka EM)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1393336-jennifer-aka-em"><img alt="Jennifer (aka EM)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510513814p2/1393336.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/224836687?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer (aka EM)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer (aka EM)" href="/user/show/1393336-jennifer-aka-em">Jennifer (aka EM)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/6484819-love-medicine">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1393336-jennifer-aka-em?shelf=idle-no-more">idle-no-more</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer224836687" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7002258039069359794">This is the short story collection (some call it a novel) that launches the community of characters Erdrich will revisit through another five (six?) books - a form that seems entirely her own. As she says in this "newly revised" edition: "Since writing <i>Love Medicine</i>, I have understood that I am writing one long book in which the main chapters are also books titled <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/78887.Tracks' title='Tracks by Louise Erdrich' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tracks</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/91437.Four_Souls' title='Four Souls by Louise Erdrich' rel='nofollow noopener'>Four Souls</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/195326.The_Bingo_Palace' title='The Bingo Palace by Louise Erdrich' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Bingo Palace</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/63837.The_Last_Report_On_The_Miracles_At_Little_No_Horse' title='The Last Report On The Miracles At Little No Horse by Louise Erdrich' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Last Report on the Miracles at Little No Horse</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52528.The_Painted_Drum' title='The Painted Drum by Louise Erdrich' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Painted Drum</a>."<br/><br/>One of the things I lo</span>
  <span id="freeText7002258039069359794" style="display:none">This is the short story collection (some call it a novel) that launches the community of characters Erdrich will revisit through another five (six?) books - a form that seems entirely her own. As she says in this "newly revised" edition: "Since writing <i>Love Medicine</i>, I have understood that I am writing one long book in which the main chapters are also books titled <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/78887.Tracks" title="Tracks by Louise Erdrich" rel="nofollow noopener">Tracks</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/91437.Four_Souls" title="Four Souls by Louise Erdrich" rel="nofollow noopener">Four Souls</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/195326.The_Bingo_Palace" title="The Bingo Palace by Louise Erdrich" rel="nofollow noopener">The Bingo Palace</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/63837.The_Last_Report_On_The_Miracles_At_Little_No_Horse" title="The Last Report On The Miracles At Little No Horse by Louise Erdrich" rel="nofollow noopener">The Last Report on the Miracles at Little No Horse</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/52528.The_Painted_Drum" title="The Painted Drum by Louise Erdrich" rel="nofollow noopener">The Painted Drum</a>."<br /><br />One of the things I love most about the Erdrich I've read / am reading (in ass-backward order), is the fluidity of the form; how it's almost secondary to the stories she's telling and characters she's creating. These characters have such presence, such a voice (and here, their uniqueness is loud and clear, with each having his or her own 'space') that they feel as though they are springing fully-formed to life. As though they have been there for eons, just awaiting their turn to speak through whatever medium is available to them or that their creator chooses in the moment. It feels, as Michelangelo said of his sculptures, that the artist is simply liberating them whole from the marble or in Erdrich's case, from North Dakota land, lake, rock, and trees (and from a world that is pristine, alternative, past but still present, in contrast to the one in which we, i.e., conquering white culture, live).<br /><br />But all of this is to intellectualize and miss the point of what, at root, is a remarkable set of linked short stories of such power, originality, poetry and range that they will take your breath away. The second, <i>Saint Marie</i>, might be my favourite, with its portrayal of psychopathological religious conviction; although <i>A Bridge</i>, more subtle, is equally amazing as an exploration of PTSD; <i>Resurrection</i>, one of the shortest, feels like a raw wound of mother's love and the pain of addiction; and the titular <i>Love Medicine</i> is incredible for a variety of reasons, but in particular, the humour.<br /><br />Read them all.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7002258039069359794" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3570758151" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3570758151" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565547225p2/11255849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3570758151?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=2020-reads">2020-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=love">love</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3570758151" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer952138989489558158"><i>What's your love medicine?</i> If only you could give a person <i>love medicine</i> today and keep them safe from the virus, keep them safe from the hate, keep their heart fastened to yours. Alas, even Marie Kashpaw saw that things don't necessarily work out this way, at least not in ways we imagine. <br/><br/>The heart is deceitful to even the person who owns it, as most of these characters learn. Each person carries a deep need for transformation, an urgency to do something more, even if it means marrying the wron</span>
  <span id="freeText952138989489558158" style="display:none"><i>What's your love medicine?</i> If only you could give a person <i>love medicine</i> today and keep them safe from the virus, keep them safe from the hate, keep their heart fastened to yours. Alas, even Marie Kashpaw saw that things don't necessarily work out this way, at least not in ways we imagine. <br /><br />The heart is deceitful to even the person who owns it, as most of these characters learn. Each person carries a deep need for transformation, an urgency to do something more, even if it means marrying the wrong person. Each character seems to escape a traumatic past or seek a better future. <br /><br />The novel follows Native American families: the Kashpaws and Lamartines. Louise Erdrich's mother is half Ojibwe and Erdrich is an enrolled member of the Anishinaabe nation. Her characters are also Chippewa. Although stark, the male narratives were introspective. Nector Kashpaw, Chippewa Chairman, was one of my favorites. <br /><br />As for the female narratives, Erdrich waxes her females so lyrical that sometimes I got lost in the music of their words, followed their thoughts eagerly if only to hear the sound their words made:<br /><br /><b>
  <blockquote>I want to grind men's bones to drink in my night tea. I want to enter them the way their hot shadows fold into their bodies in full sunlight. I want to be their food, their harmful drink, to taste men like stilled jam at the back of my tongue.</blockquote>
</b><br /><br />These linked stories were a joy to read in Erdrich's unique, lyrical style. Some of the paragraphs are like poems, abstract, with sentences reading like verses. Some of the stories are from perspectives and voices so very different from others. The style is varied, just like the characters. Time is elusive. I swooned over some portions but found myself getting lost at the halfway point, especially when time traversed two decades, the story style changed, and I struggled to attach a character with a past story. At times I found myself skipping pages. The first part was definitely my favorite. What I enjoyed most about this novel was getting lost in the urgency of each character, watching heartbreak turn into love and vice versa, escaping into the frenzy of their lives.</span>
  <a data-text-id="952138989489558158" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1791483775" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1791483775" />
    <a title="El" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83144-el"><img alt="El" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479156984p2/83144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1791483775?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="El" class="user" itemprop="url" name="El" href="/user/show/83144-el">El</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780805027983" href="/book/show/1266898.Love_Medicine">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=1001-books-list">1001-books-list</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=biddles">biddles</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=20th-centurylit-late">20th-centurylit-late</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=cultural-studies-and-other">cultural-studies-and-other</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=native-american">native-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1791483775" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1969145847389264100">I recently read Erdrich's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/106542.The_Antelope_Wife' title='The Antelope Wife by Louise Erdrich' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Antelope Wife</a> and really fucking loved it. I am now planning on reading everything by Erdrich I can - luckily for me she has written quite a bit over the years, so I can take my time and maybe not even run out of material.<br/><br/><i>Love Medicine</i> was her first novel, published in the mid-80s. This one, I understand, was revised and expanded at least a couple times since it's original publication date. One might think that must be hard to do, and I think in a traditional novel </span>
  <span id="freeText1969145847389264100" style="display:none">I recently read Erdrich's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/106542.The_Antelope_Wife" title="The Antelope Wife by Louise Erdrich" rel="nofollow noopener">The Antelope Wife</a> and really fucking loved it. I am now planning on reading everything by Erdrich I can - luckily for me she has written quite a bit over the years, so I can take my time and maybe not even run out of material.<br /><br /><i>Love Medicine</i> was her first novel, published in the mid-80s. This one, I understand, was revised and expanded at least a couple times since it's original publication date. One might think that must be hard to do, and I think in a traditional novel it would be difficult to piece together a cohesive story over the years. But you will find with Erdrich (at least with the whopping two experiences I now have with her) that her stories already are sort of a patchwork quilt to begin with, so adding material over time would not complicate anything. Nothing gets lost along the way. It's practically, I think, seamless.<br /><br />The story is about a group of Chippewa on a reservation in North Dakota, spanning a period of about 60 years. As with <i>The Antelope Wife</i>, I started reading this with a bit of trepidation. Each books start with one character, or one perspective, and then the next chapter is another, and so on. Time jumps throughout history (though those chapters are clearly defined so you're not <i>entirely</i> lost, but it can take a bit to acclimate oneself to the switch), perspectives change with the characters. Personally, I love reading stories that are told through the eyes of different characters, for I'm a huge proponent of the whole "There's three sides to every story - my side, your side, and then the truth", so when an author is able to portray exactly that without making me hate any of the characters, I find that pretty powerful and skilled.<br /><br />I will not say I made a connection to every character. It is difficult (especially in <i>Love Medicine</i>) to keep track of who is related to whom and the lines of history which cross and crisscross throughout the book. Erdrich does expect her readers to pay attention. Some editions I have seen have a family tree - my copy, a book club edition that claims to be "new and expanded version", does <i>not</i> have a family tree. What I understand, however, is that those with the family tree didn't have much better luck keeping track of everyone anyway. My point is, it's just not easy, and that's okay because a family history is never that easy.<br /><br />Erdrich has a way with words that really appeals to me. I wish I could be as eloquent and articulate in anything I write, but this is why she is a master that is beloved by pretty much everyone I have ever met who has read her.<br /><br />I will slowly make my way through Erdrich's oeuvre, but not all at once because I think that would be overwhelming. I feel her writing is the sort that deserves to be savored, and should not be a binge-read. She deserves our attention for her portrayal of Native Americans without being condescending, patronizing, or flippant. She recognizes the very real problems that have occurred over the years, and continue to occur. Her thoughts, for example, on the pipe line right now would especially interest me; I hope to one day read her thoughts on the matter, or a book about the experiences of those it affects the most (though it affects us all, let's not forget that).<br /><br />"The red-eyed moths had come out of the trees where they hid themselves, looking exactly like dead lives. Drawn by the bright flames, they'd come helplessly to burn." (p286)<br /><br />I mean, wow.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1969145847389264100" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer364669930" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4130201723740756586">What a fantastic debut novel! It has linking stories from different Ojibwe people in different generations, but not only do they tell a shared story, they interlink with the books we already read - Tracks and Four Souls - even though those books were written later. I did read the 1993 revised edition. <br/><br/>One element I loved was the portrayal of people in love even in old age. Here is a quotation that speaks to that: “I thought love got easier over the years so it didn’t hurt so bad when it hurt, or</span>
  <span id="freeText4130201723740756586" style="display:none">What a fantastic debut novel! It has linking stories from different Ojibwe people in different generations, but not only do they tell a shared story, they interlink with the books we already read - Tracks and Four Souls - even though those books were written later. I did read the 1993 revised edition. <br /><br />One element I loved was the portrayal of people in love even in old age. Here is a quotation that speaks to that: “I thought love got easier over the years so it didn’t hurt so bad when it hurt, or feel so good when it felt good. I thought it smoothed out and old people hardly noticed it. I thought it curled up and died...Now I saw it rear up like a whip and a lash. She loved him. She was jealous. She mourned him like the dead."<br /><br />Highly recommended, but you can't rush this one. Everything connects.</span>

    <div class="updateActionLinks">
        <a class="right flag" rel="nofollow" id="flag_link364669930" title="Flag this review as inappropriate." href="/flagged/new?resource_id=364669930&amp;resource_type=Review&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F91440.Love_Medicine">flag</a>

<div id="review_1935349284" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1935349284" />
    <a title="Ann Girdharry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13002277-ann-girdharry"><img alt="Ann Girdharry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444634224p2/13002277.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1935349284?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ann Girdharry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ann Girdharry" href="/user/show/13002277-ann-girdharry">Ann Girdharry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13002277-ann-girdharry?shelf=oss-main-character-of-colour">oss-main-character-of-colour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13002277-ann-girdharry?shelf=my-challenge-authors-of-colour">my-challenge-authors-of-colour</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1935349284" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16271853636794605837">This is such a great book.<br/>It's also a very difficult one to read because it pulls no punches about the Native American experience. In this book you will read about grindingly cruel experiences, the drudgery of daily life, alcoholism and suffering, in-fighting and rivalry that lasts generations. <br/><br/>Erdrich tells us about her characters in small stories, each centred around a different character. Sometimes we read about the same event in different stories, told from different perspectives or perhaps </span>
  <span id="freeText16271853636794605837" style="display:none">This is such a great book.<br />It's also a very difficult one to read because it pulls no punches about the Native American experience. In this book you will read about grindingly cruel experiences, the drudgery of daily life, alcoholism and suffering, in-fighting and rivalry that lasts generations. <br /><br />Erdrich tells us about her characters in small stories, each centred around a different character. Sometimes we read about the same event in different stories, told from different perspectives or perhaps by someone in the succeeding generation. <br /><br />Erdrich tells us about her characters by telling the story of key points in their everyday lives - cooking for guests, caring for a loved-one in an alcoholic stupor, greeting relatives, losing a relative, visiting a forbidden lover, coming home from war. <br />In these short scenes, she describes her characters so fully, so completely, that we see them bared to the bones of who they are. Yet in telling us of their weaknesses she does not diminish them. <br /><br />I think this is what I loved the most about this book because somehow her writing has the opposite effect and lifts her characters up. <br /><br />It's Erdrich's actual writing style that conveys this special touch and brings beauty and eloquence to it all. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16271853636794605837" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_981967787" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/981967787" />
    <a title="Connie G" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5364077-connie-g"><img alt="Connie G" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307502493p2/5364077.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/981967787?book_show_action=true">Jun 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Connie G" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Connie G" href="/user/show/5364077-connie-g">Connie G</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=north-dakota">north-dakota</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=native-american-or-canadian">native-american-or-canadian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=year-of-reading-women-authors">year-of-reading-women-authors</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer981967787" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7546125472243392785">"Love Medicine" is a multigenerational novel about two interrelated families living on a North Dakota reservation from the 1930s to the 1980s. It's written as a series of 18 interlocked stories that often tell about the same situation from a second character's point of view. Native American myths and tricksters color the stories. The author uses wonderful imagery involving water, fire, bridges, and religion. The characters are very conflicted, hanging on to old traditions while living in a moder</span>
  <span id="freeText7546125472243392785" style="display:none">"Love Medicine" is a multigenerational novel about two interrelated families living on a North Dakota reservation from the 1930s to the 1980s. It's written as a series of 18 interlocked stories that often tell about the same situation from a second character's point of view. Native American myths and tricksters color the stories. The author uses wonderful imagery involving water, fire, bridges, and religion. The characters are very conflicted, hanging on to old traditions while living in a modern world. Poverty, abuse, and alcoholism are major problems. There is a real sense of family love from the two matriarchs of the Kashpaws and the Lamartines. These complex characters are brought to life through Louise Erdrich's exceptional writing. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7546125472243392785" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3304266881" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3304266881" />
    <a title="Rebecca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5875398-rebecca"><img alt="Rebecca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599578317p2/5875398.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3304266881?book_show_action=true">May 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca" href="/user/show/5875398-rebecca">Rebecca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780708828595" href="/book/show/3281127-love-medicine">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=linked-short-stories">linked-short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=university-library">university-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3304266881" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14453929902009441694">This was an excellent introduction to Erdrich’s work. (I’m definitely going to pretend that that <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2047985887' rel='nofollow noopener'>Future Home of the Living God</a> DNF never happened.) I made the foolish mistake of not taking any notes or writing this up at the time, so four months later I have retained precious few details. To start with, I had a little trouble keeping the characters and chronology straight, but before long I was captivated by these interlocking stories that span half a century in the lives of a couple of Chippewa</span>
  <span id="freeText14453929902009441694" style="display:none">This was an excellent introduction to Erdrich’s work. (I’m definitely going to pretend that that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2047985887" rel="nofollow noopener">Future Home of the Living God</a> DNF never happened.) I made the foolish mistake of not taking any notes or writing this up at the time, so four months later I have retained precious few details. To start with, I had a little trouble keeping the characters and chronology straight, but before long I was captivated by these interlocking stories that span half a century in the lives of a couple of Chippewa families that sprawl out from a North Dakota reservation. Erdrich gives you all the clues you need, labeling each story with the year and the narrator or POV character, and there are so many larger-than-life figures here that it’s easy to remember them from one story to the next. Surnames like Kashpaw, Lamartine and Nanapush. Looking for love, looking for work. Getting lucky, getting even. The problems aren’t specific to Native Americans, just the stuff of human nature and contemporary life (adultery, alcoholism, estrangement; loss of faith in both God and the government).<br /><br />I adored the descriptions of characters and of nature – two favorite passages were: <br /><blockquote>“By that time Dot weighed over two hundred pounds, most of it peanut-butter cups and egg-salad sandwiches. She was a short, broad-beamed woman with long yellow eyes and spaces between each of her strong teeth.”<br /><br />“At times the whole sky was ringed in shooting points and puckers of light gathering and falling, pulsing, fading, rhythmical as breathing. All of a piece. As if the sky were a pattern of nerves and our thought and memories traveled across it. As if the sky were one gigantic memory for us all. Or a dance hall. And all the world’s wandering souls were dancing there.”</blockquote><br />My favorite individual story was “Saint Marie,” set at Sacred Heart Convent in the 1930s. My favorite single line was “Belonging was a matter of deciding to.”<br /><br />[I find it interesting that Erdrich extensively revised this book over the years; to me it was pretty much perfect, and I can’t wait to read more of her work. (I read a 1985 paperback published in the UK by Futura, complete with a dated back cover blurb reference to “Red Indian families” (yipes!) and a puff attributed to “Ann Tyler” (oh dear).)]</span>
  <a data-text-id="14453929902009441694" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1216466796" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1216466796" />
    <a title="Holly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7240219-holly"><img alt="Holly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466886310p2/7240219.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1216466796?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Holly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Holly" href="/user/show/7240219-holly">Holly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1216466796" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1342684871137894295">A remarkable report from Chippewa country. I finally <i>get</i> Erdrich in a way that <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13602426.The_Round_House' title='The Round House by Louise Erdrich' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Round House</a>, with all of its successes, failed to grab me. I have read from many Erdrich fans how she's an author whose books they read over and over; I am not a big re-reader, even of my favorite books—I re-read passages and lines, and a cherished favorite once in a blue moon, but there's so little time and too many books—but while reading this, I understood her fans' (and I can now count myself as one of them) i</span>
  <span id="freeText1342684871137894295" style="display:none">A remarkable report from Chippewa country. I finally <i>get</i> Erdrich in a way that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13602426.The_Round_House" title="The Round House by Louise Erdrich" rel="nofollow noopener">The Round House</a>, with all of its successes, failed to grab me. I have read from many Erdrich fans how she's an author whose books they read over and over; I am not a big re-reader, even of my favorite books—I re-read passages and lines, and a cherished favorite once in a blue moon, but there's so little time and too many books—but while reading this, I understood her fans' (and I can now count myself as one of them) insistence on traversing her words over and over again. Her language is elegant and storied without pretense, and her characters and their lives are developed like perfectly-portioned slices of cake. Erdrich's books remind non-Natives that Natives are not dead or mythical or Other, but human beings struggling to make their way like everyone else (with, perhaps, a different set of parameters and restrictions and ghosts bearing down, but inhabiting this planet and its cities and its technologies and its heartbreaks, too).</span>
  <a data-text-id="1342684871137894295" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tori (InToriLex)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tori (InToriLex)" href="/user/show/10584748-tori-intorilex">Tori (InToriLex)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2104916537" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15298473747404767756">Find this and other Reviews at <a href='http://www.intorilex.com' rel='nofollow noopener'>In Tori Lex</a><br/><br/>This is a memorable family saga that depicts the many hardships Native American Families have faced. This novel follows the Kapshaw and Nanapush families over from the 1930's to the 1980's. Nector Kapshaw binds two families together because he maintains an affair with a woman while married. Both women love him despite of it. This book describes the family drama, tragedy and alcoholism that afflicts members of the family. Each character shared a unique an</span>
  <span id="freeText15298473747404767756" style="display:none">Find this and other Reviews at <a href="http://www.intorilex.com" rel="nofollow noopener">In Tori Lex</a><br /><br />This is a memorable family saga that depicts the many hardships Native American Families have faced. This novel follows the Kapshaw and Nanapush families over from the 1930's to the 1980's. Nector Kapshaw binds two families together because he maintains an affair with a woman while married. Both women love him despite of it. This book describes the family drama, tragedy and alcoholism that afflicts members of the family. Each character shared a unique and engaging glimpse into their lives. The character development was phenomenal and interspersed with vivid imagery and prose.<br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  
    <b>"Rushes Bear always said that a man has to enter and enter, repeatedly, as if in punishment for having ever left the woman's body."</b>
  
</blockquote><br />Despite the heartbreaking experiences characters face their reverence to nature and distrust of white people was well founded. The women in this book are not passive observers to the addiction and infidelity around them. They hold their families together, acknowledging the bad but still finding ways to love and care for the family around them. Since there is rampant infidelity many of the families on the reservation are loosely related.The reservation itself is a community that relies on each other. <br /><blockquote>
  <br />
  
    <b>What aggravates them is I've never shed one solitary tear. I'm not sorry. That's unnatural. As we all know, a woman is supposed to cry.</b>
  
</blockquote><br />The Native Americans beliefs and traditions were well described.I'm excited to read more books from this author in the future to learn more about the Native American way of life. It's <br />important to remember the issues that continue to plague Native American<br /> reservations. This story enabled me to engage with the realities of poverty and frequent alcoholism in a memorable way. I was frequently emotional over what was being described, but I happily ravished this book. The writing was lyrical thought provoking and amazing overall. <br /><br /><b>Recommended For Readers:</b><br />-who enjoy family sagas<br />-who want to learn more about Native American Communities<br />-who enjoy emotionally charged contemporaries </span>
  <a data-text-id="15298473747404767756" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1376727543" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1376727543" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23841736-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532221628p2/23841736.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1376727543?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/23841736-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/6484819-love-medicine">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23841736-sarah?shelf=2017-classics-challenge">2017-classics-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1376727543" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12684766158902614072"><i>So many things in the world have happened before. But it's like they never did. Every new thing that happens to a person, it's a first... In that night I felt expansion, as if the world was branching out in shoots and growing faster than the eye could see. I felt smallness, how the earthy divided into bits and kept dividing. I felt the stars. I felt them roosting on my shoulders with his hand. The moon came up red and warm.</i><br/><br/>I first read this book in a Native American lit college course in 2000 an</span>
  <span id="freeText12684766158902614072" style="display:none"><i>So many things in the world have happened before. But it's like they never did. Every new thing that happens to a person, it's a first... In that night I felt expansion, as if the world was branching out in shoots and growing faster than the eye could see. I felt smallness, how the earthy divided into bits and kept dividing. I felt the stars. I felt them roosting on my shoulders with his hand. The moon came up red and warm.</i><br /><br />I first read this book in a Native American lit college course in 2000 and my love of the book meant that I read her books whenever I ran across them. It was also the basis of my Native American Lit challenge for this year.<br /><br />I didn't love it quite as much this time as I did last, but I still enjoyed it a lot. It's about two families on a Chipewa reservation, the Nanapush and Lamartine families, as well as a few others because Lulu had a lot of love to give and therefore a lot of kids. I never would have tracked this book without the family tree thingy at the beginning. <br /><br />The story takes place over about 60 years and is told from many POVs with a large number of the extended family members getting a chapter or two. Some of the characters get more chapters but all of the characters have excellent character development. After some jumping around at the beginning the story progresses forward in time even as it jumps from family member to family member. It's a really good story and I'm hoping to move on to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/91439.The_Beet_Queen" title="The Beet Queen by Louise Erdrich" rel="nofollow noopener">The Beet Queen</a> soon. This is listed as a sequel to Love Medicine but it appears to merely take place in the town the reservation uses.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_6756689" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6756689" />
    <a title="Lanea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/285644-lanea"><img alt="Lanea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1237378978p2/285644.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6756689?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lanea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lanea" href="/user/show/285644-lanea">Lanea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/285644-lanea?shelf=women-writers">women-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/285644-lanea?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6756689" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17316724317001888347">The novel is set largely on a Chippewa reservation in North Dakota, with brief forays to the Twin Cities. There is a family tree at the beginning of the book--refer to it as you read. This is essentially the story of two linked multi-generational families. The speaker shifts from chapter to chapter, as does the point in the time-line. Now we have the voice of a young student going home to visit her grandparents and worrying about her cousin, now the voice of that grandmother still a young woman,</span>
  <span id="freeText17316724317001888347" style="display:none">The novel is set largely on a Chippewa reservation in North Dakota, with brief forays to the Twin Cities. There is a family tree at the beginning of the book--refer to it as you read. This is essentially the story of two linked multi-generational families. The speaker shifts from chapter to chapter, as does the point in the time-line. Now we have the voice of a young student going home to visit her grandparents and worrying about her cousin, now the voice of that grandmother still a young woman, explaining her choice of mates. <br /><br />What really matters, throughout, is Erdrich's beautiful way with language. She accomplishes something all too rare--she writes in the voice of an uneducated person who is brilliant. We don't see that often. Many writers can write as an ignoramus--just mess up their grammar and give them small words and a character will seem dumb. But to write as someone without great facility for language who is simultaneously brilliant, prescient, and uneducated is quite an accomplishment. We see Lipsha's intelligence shimmer on the page, despite his clumsiness with English. <br /><br />We also receive a cast of characters that are complex, flawed, and still loveable. We feel somewhere the urge to hate Lulu or Nector for their selfishness and deceit, and yet we forgive them their sins. We admire Marie, but know that her own selflessness also hides some greed that would injure a marriage. I don't often run across a writer that can make so many characters so well-developed and completely flawed but also completely attractive. I'll keep reading Erdrich. If her later work is even better, I will feel like a very lucky reader.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17316724317001888347" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
